  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_bf7c9ab3-ec73-4272-8db5-39fdb3d97156'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

<span id="freeTextContainer2809790130335990588">In the 1680s the slave trade in the Americas is still in its infancy. Jacob Vaark is an Anglo-Dutch trader and adventurer, with a small holding in the harsh North. Despite his distaste for dealing in “flesh,” he takes a small slave girl in part payment for a bad debt from a plantation owner in Catholic Maryland. <br/>This is Florens, who can read and write and might be useful o</span>
  <span id="freeText2809790130335990588" style="display:none">In the 1680s the slave trade in the Americas is still in its infancy. Jacob Vaark is an Anglo-Dutch trader and adventurer, with a small holding in the harsh North. Despite his distaste for dealing in “flesh,” he takes a small slave girl in part payment for a bad debt from a plantation owner in Catholic Maryland. <br />This is Florens, who can read and write and might be useful on his farm. Rejected by her mother, Florens looks for love, first from Lina, an older servant woman at her new master's house, and later from the handsome blacksmith, an African, never enslaved, who comes riding into their lives.<br />A Mercy reveals what lies beneath the surface of slavery. But at its heart, like Beloved, it is the ambivalent, disturbing story of a mother and a daughter - a mother who casts off her daughter in order to save her, and a daughter who may never exorcise that abandonment.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2809790130335990588" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_41566043" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41566043" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41566043?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer41566043&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41566043" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10230878329666816552">This is a devastating look not only at the slave experience in the 17th century, but of various forms of bondage and of the place of women in that world. While skin color may have defined one sort of servitude, gender and class define others. Yet there are ways to find space between the bars. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448000385i/17080508.jpg' width='400' height='xxx' alt='description' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><b>Toni Morrison</b> - from The Telegraph<br/><br/>Set in the late 17th century, this is an ensemble story. Florens is a young, spirited slave girl. She speaks in the first person giving us a look from inside her skin. Exce</span>
  <span id="freeText10230878329666816552" style="display:none">This is a devastating look not only at the slave experience in the 17th century, but of various forms of bondage and of the place of women in that world. While skin color may have defined one sort of servitude, gender and class define others. Yet there are ways to find space between the bars. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1448000385i/17080508.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Toni Morrison</b> - from The Telegraph<br><br>Set in the late 17th century, this is an ensemble story. Florens is a young, spirited slave girl. She speaks in the first person giving us a look from inside her skin. Except for one chapter by Florens’ mother, the rest of the book is third person. We see Florens’ experience with owners, families and yearnings old and new. Jakob is a kindly merchant/farmer, eager to help the unfortunate, always recalling his own humble, even desperate childhood. Rebekka is outwardly a free white woman, but truly was sold by her parents to be Jakob’s wife no less than any black slave. Lina is the eldest of three helper women on Jakob’s farm, a mother and friend to Rebekka. She is a Native American, a survivor of a small pox outbreak and the subsequent torching of her village. Sorrow is a black-toothed, somewhat addled woman who washed up on shore after a shipwreck. She holds conversations with an imaginary twin. Morrison also shows us two indentured servants. Despite having fixed terms to their indenture, their bosses find or concoct excuses to prolong their indebtedness and thus their servitude endlessly.<blockquote>
  <b>the original seven years stretched to twenty-some, he said, and he had long ago forgotten most of the mischief that kept extending his bondage.</b>
</blockquote> One cannot but think of today’s extension of tours of duty in the military.<br><br>Religion comes in for a beating here.<blockquote>
  <b>Religion, as Rebekka experienced if from her mother, was a flame fueled by a wondrous hatred.</b>
</blockquote>In one scene, Florens is suspected by a bizarre woman of being a witch and is forced to strip so she can be checked to see if she has a tail. Later, she attaches herself to a free black man in another form of devotion, only to have that work out badly. Lina had been taken in by a group of fundamentalists, who, unkindly, named her Messalina, seeing her as cursed by God. A curate is guilty of buggering a young boy.<br><br>The 17th century is a cruel, harsh world<blockquote>
  <b>…it was clear in her household that execution was a festivity as exciting as a king’s parade. Brawls, knifings and kidnaps were so common in the city of her birth that the warnings of slaughter in a new, unseen world were like threats of bad weather</b>
</blockquote> both in the old world and the new. Jakob offers a relatively safe place, a fenced-in peaceful community where orphans collect.<br><br>But even with all the horror of the world we still define for ourselves who we are, maybe by paring crusted misery from our memory, maybe from holding some part of our true self separate from the harsh world.<blockquote>
  <b>Solitude, regret and fury would have broken her had she not erased those six years preceding the death of the world. The company of other children, industrious mothers in beautiful jewelry, the majestic plan of life: when to vacate, to harvest, to burn, to hunt; ceremonies of death, birth and worship. She stored and sorted what she dared to recall and eliminated the rest, an activity that shaped her inside and out. By the time Mistress came, her self-invention was almost perfected. Soon it was irresistible.</b>
</blockquote>While maybe not the masterpiece that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39662878" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a> was, <i>A Mercy</i> is a very dense novel, a torte of a work, covering a range of subjects rich in significance, in language that is moving and penetrating.<br><br>=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br><br>Morrison’s <a href="https://www.facebook.com/OfficialToniMorrisonAuthor" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a> page - Morrison passed in 2019. The page is maintained by Knopf.<br><br><b>Reviews of other Morrison work</b><br>-----2014 - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1275734575" rel="nofollow noopener">God Help the Child</a><br>-----2011 - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/387465067" rel="nofollow noopener">Home</a><br>-----1987 - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39662878" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a><br><br><b>Read but not reviewed</b><br>-----1977 - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11334.Song_of_Solomon" rel="nofollow noopener">Song of Solomon</a><br>-----1973 - <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11346.Sula" rel="nofollow noopener">Sula</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10230878329666816552" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1050815120" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1050815120" />
    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1050815120?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowena" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2990642-rowena?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1050815120" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13432806477257378874"> <b> “It was there I learned how I was not a person from my country, nor from my families. I was negrita. Everything. Language, dress, gods, dance, habits, decoration, song– all of it cooked together in the colour of my skin.” </b>– Toni Morrison, A Mercy<br/><br/>It’s the 17th Century, and slavery is still relatively new in the Americas. The people living there have either been brought there by force or have voluntarily gone there to start a new life. They are people with no roots in their new country, no famil</span>
  <span id="freeText13432806477257378874" style="display:none"> <b> “It was there I learned how I was not a person from my country, nor from my families. I was negrita. Everything. Language, dress, gods, dance, habits, decoration, song– all of it cooked together in the colour of my skin.” </b>– Toni Morrison, A Mercy<br /><br />It’s the 17th Century, and slavery is still relatively new in the Americas. The people living there have either been brought there by force or have voluntarily gone there to start a new life. They are people with no roots in their new country, no family either. And in such a small space, Morrison manages to pack away so many stories, so many emotions.<br /><br />This was definitely a satisfying read and, as is always the case with Morrison, we are rewarded with poetic and perfectly crafted sentences.<br /><br />Morrison paints the New World as a place of hope, but also very different from the Old World:<br /><br /><i>“Right, he thought, looking at a sky vulgar with stars. Clear and right. The silver that glittered there was not at all unreachable. And that wide swath of cream pouring through the stars was his for the tasting.”</i><br /><br /><i>“Unlike the English fogs he had known since he could walk, or those way north where he lived now, this one was sun fired, turning the world into thick, hot gold. Penetrating it was like struggling through a dream.”</i><br /><br />What’s common among the women in the story is that they are all victims. Regardless of colour or station, be they slave or free, they are reliant on men and their situation is precarious. They are in many ways pawns; their role in the New World is only slightly above cattle.<br /><br />How do people displaced from their homes try to come to terms with this separation? How do they try to save their cultures and traditions?<br /><br /><i>“Relying on memory and her own resources, she cobbled together neglected rites, merged Europe medicine with native, scripture with lore, and recalled or invented the hidden meaning of things. Found, in other words, a way to be in the world.”</i><br /><br />Slavery is never easy to read about but this line was especially poignant to me:<br /><br /> <i> “The two men walked the row, inspecting. D’Ortega identifying talents, weaknesses and possibilities, but silent about the scars, the wounds like misplaced veins tracing their skin.”</i><br /><br />This book has a lot of sadness in it, then again Morrison’s books are never cheerful. What I love about her chronicles is that she gives voices and feelings to people who are often ignored. Morrison always seems to adds a new layer of emotion to her characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13432806477257378874" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_216979850" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216979850" />
    <a title="Jason Koivu" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu"><img alt="Jason Koivu" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560521450p2/1742824.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216979850?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jason Koivu" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jason Koivu" href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu">Jason Koivu</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216979850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9019076742490130288">Toni, Toni, Toni...it feels good to know you again.<br/><br/><i>A Mercy</i> is a gorgeous narrative of a dark time that flitters from person to person: child, slave, sympathetic Dutch businessman, mother. Betrayal is ever present, even seemingly from mother to child.<br/><br/>The setting and subject is slavery in 17th century America, specifically Catholic Maryland. These are early days in the New World. Superstition was rife. Black magic and the devil were palpably real. <br/><br/>With a bevy of glimpses Morrison displays most of </span>
  <span id="freeText9019076742490130288" style="display:none">Toni, Toni, Toni...it feels good to know you again.<br /><br /><i>A Mercy</i> is a gorgeous narrative of a dark time that flitters from person to person: child, slave, sympathetic Dutch businessman, mother. Betrayal is ever present, even seemingly from mother to child.<br /><br />The setting and subject is slavery in 17th century America, specifically Catholic Maryland. These are early days in the New World. Superstition was rife. Black magic and the devil were palpably real. <br /><br />With a bevy of glimpses Morrison displays most of the facets of slavery in this period, in this place. She does not forget that it was black Africans who kidnapped and sold black Africans to white Europeans, who sold them into slavery. She did not forget that white slavery existed in this time. She wrote about a people's strife without bended knee and bleeding heart, and yet your heart will bleed.<br /><br />Admittedly, I was turned off within the first few pages, because of the gypsy narration. I like permanence in my storytelling voice and this was very reminiscent of William Faulkner's <i>The Sound and The Fury</i> with its variant and confusing p.o.v.s and its scene setting via murky imagery. But I stuck with it, soon was enjoying <i>A Mercy</i> and in the end, came to love it.<br /><br />The writing is so strong, emotive and filled with vivid imagery. It is the kind of writing that inspires writers in their craft.<br /><br />This was a revisit to an old acquaintance for me. Not since college have I read a Toni Morrison novel. I loved it then, so why the delay? Why do we do that? When you only have one life - a single existence which could be snuffed out in an instance - why neglect the good things in life? Cherish what you have. It may be taken from you. Though we can only hope fate will be merciful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9019076742490130288" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_41150671" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/41150671" />
    <a title="Jessica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/419287-jessica"><img alt="Jessica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1289534648p2/419287.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/41150671?book_show_action=true">Dec 28, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessica" href="/user/show/419287-jessica">Jessica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer41150671" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16963923102586753230">Back in college I took a course on Colonial America because I had to. It was pretty tough for me to get into it at the time, since I never really gave a crap about that inaccessible and unglamorous period. I wish this book had been around in those days, because Morrison's efforts to describe that bizarre and confusing world might've helped me get better picture of the time, and therefore care more about what I was learning. To me, <i>A Mercy</i> really is incredible historical fiction that provides acc</span>
  <span id="freeText16963923102586753230" style="display:none">Back in college I took a course on Colonial America because I had to. It was pretty tough for me to get into it at the time, since I never really gave a crap about that inaccessible and unglamorous period. I wish this book had been around in those days, because Morrison's efforts to describe that bizarre and confusing world might've helped me get better picture of the time, and therefore care more about what I was learning. To me, <i>A Mercy</i> really is incredible historical fiction that provides access to a strange time and the people who lived in it. Morrison exposes the insides of her seventeenth-century characters in a way that made them sympathetic and comprehensible, though still remote and unfamiliar, even foreign. I didn't feel I could relate to these people at all, which for me was part of the point -- what do I have in common with someone living four hundred years ago? -- but also kept me feeling somewhat detached for the first two thirds of the novel. I enjoyed it, but I didn't really freak out about this book the way some other Booksters did until the last few chapters, which were totally fabulous and really did blow my mind.<br /><br />Recently on here there was some friendly (I think) bickering about Howard Zinn's <i>People's History of the US</i> and the whole question of multiculturalism and revisionist history. Although it's fiction, <i>A Mercy</i> is an almost perfect example of how I like my American history served: organic, unflinching, and successfully synthesizing, rather than just reacting against previous histories. In <i>A Mercy,</i> Morrison tears away the veil of idealism and whitewashing to stare unflinchingly at the myth of America, then unapologetically celebrates that very myth (it seemed to me), and it's pretty extraordinary. There's one passage in here about a character leaving the cramped, violent filth of Europe for the fresh, clean, virginal Eden of the New World, and it just gave me <i>chills.</i> I mean, it's really spectacular! She writes about all this slavery and sexual violence and persecution and disease in a way that lets you imagine what it was like to live in that era, and somehow, paradoxically, it makes the classic American myths -- especially the myth of freedom -- seem true for the first time, in a new way.... if that makes any sense. Morrison captures the complexity and brutality of Colonial America, cataloguing the various ways there were to be unfree (slavery, indentured servitude, marriage, etc.) through a multiethnic cast of characters who she's able to write as both weighty symbols and deeply human individuals in a way only <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Toni Morrison</a> -- and almost no one else -- is able to get away with. The chapters are written from the perspectives of the various characters, in this unbelievable Morrisonesque language that will make you freak out. Yeah, this book probably will make you freak out.... Plus, it's super short! It's also a really interesting perspective on the legacy of American slavery, that recontextualizes it in a much earlier, more northern setting than most treatments, and gets into some different questions and issues than a lot of us are probably used to. Also, did I mention this lady can <i>write?</i><br /><br />So yeah, in closing: I wasn't competely nuts about this, and especially at the beginning I had a couple weird moments where I almost felt like I was reading a trashy romance novel -- because of the subject matter, not the style -- but it was a good book, and I do recommend it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16963923102586753230" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/987010330" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2172096-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598060221p2/2172096.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/987010330?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer987010330" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10574292654554453308"><br/>This story occurs in the late 1600s, during early days of slavery in America (that is, African people being used as slaves). By that time however, the tradition of using 'indentured servants' - essentially white slaves - was already well established. <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551192941i/27121012.jpg' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>In this tale, several slaves work on a small farm run by Jacob and Rebekka Vaark.<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551192941i/27121013.png' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>The indentured servants are: Native American Lina - whose tribe has been decimated by disease; <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551192941i/27121014.png' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Black child Florens - who was given away by her mother; <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1585310847i/29179190.png' alt='' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>And jinxed Sorrow</span>
  <span id="freeText10574292654554453308" style="display:none"><br>This story occurs in the late 1600s, during early days of slavery in America (that is, African people being used as slaves). By that time however, the tradition of using 'indentured servants' - essentially white slaves - was already well established. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551192941i/27121012.jpg" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In this tale, several slaves work on a small farm run by Jacob and Rebekka Vaark.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551192941i/27121013.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The indentured servants are: Native American Lina - whose tribe has been decimated by disease; that Florens yearns for affection and fell in love with a free African blacksmith;<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1551192941i/27121018.png" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>That uneducated Sorrow - who may be more clever than she seems - still can't fathom why she keeps getting pregnant; A well-written book with compelling and interesting characters.<br><br>You can follow my reviews at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot.com/">https://reviewsbybarbsaffer.blogspot....</a></span>

<div id="review_3316556869" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3316556869" />
    <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro"><img alt="Michael Finocchiaro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576692048p2/48483884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3316556869?book_show_action=true">Jun 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael Finocchiaro" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro">Michael Finocchiaro</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=american-21st-c">american-21st-c</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=african-american-lit">african-american-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=pulitzer-fiction">pulitzer-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=slavery">slavery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3316556869" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6995332920262470531">A Mercy takes us on a voyage into the dark world of slavery in the 1690s through the eyes of several slaves and one owner. In typical Morrison style, the time is non-linear, but it is an easier book to follow than say Beloved. Its text is less powerful and poetic than say, Beloved, but still there is a poignancy and urgency to the writing. Given the current slavery-denial in the media, it is probably a timely read.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_792884493" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/792884493" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4086118-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567611029p2/4086118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/792884493?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/4086118-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=colonial-america">colonial-america</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=native-american">native-american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=maryland">maryland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=new-york">new-york</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=slavery">slavery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4086118-michael?shelf=african-american">african-american</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer792884493" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3485255310032916196">I was enthralled with the incandescent prose and moving voices of four women in this tale set on a remote farm in colonial New York in the 1690s. It was outstanding in the audiobook form read by the author, often sending chills up my spine with the vibrant power of its poetry. A major theme is how people harness love in all its forms and how they deal with the perception of betrayal. Another is the paradox of the foundation of the new world both on the hunger for freedom and on various forms of </span>
  <span id="freeText3485255310032916196" style="display:none">I was enthralled with the incandescent prose and moving voices of four women in this tale set on a remote farm in colonial New York in the 1690s. It was outstanding in the audiobook form read by the author, often sending chills up my spine with the vibrant power of its poetry. A major theme is how people harness love in all its forms and how they deal with the perception of betrayal. Another is the paradox of the foundation of the new world both on the hunger for freedom and on various forms of slavery (classic slavery, indentured servants, arranged marriage). <br /><br />The characters are wonderfully drawn. Rebekka is a “mail order” bride from England for a Dutch farmer turned trader, Jacob Vaart, whom she comes to loves dearly. Her household includes: Lina, a Native American orphaned from the decimation of her tribe by smallpox; Florens, a black slave girl whom Jacob reluctantly takes on as payment of a debt; and Sorrow, a mentally disturbed black slave who grew up on a ship of her slave trader father. Together they make a virtual family over the long intervals while Jacob is away.<br /><br />Rebekkah grieves over the loss of her children to disease and injury. She doesn’t take to the judgmental forms of Christianity in the distant town. Instead she finds community with her workers at the farm. On the ship journey to America, she surprised herself by admiring the verve and parallel outlook of fellow women passengers headed for a life of prostitution in the new world:<br /><i>What excited and challenged her shipmates horrified the churched women and each set believed the other deeply, dangerously flawed. Although they had nothing in common with the views of each other, they had everything in common with one thing: the promise and threat of men. Here, they agreed, was where security and risk lay. And both had come to terms. Some, like Lina, who had experienced both deliverance and destruction at their hands, withdrew. Some, like Sorrow, who apparently was never coached by other females, became their play. Some like her shipmates fought them. Others, the pious, obeyed them. And a few, like herself, after a mutually loving relationship, became like children when the man was gone. </i> <br /><br />The life Jacob brings her is so much freer than her life in London, but his travel to fulfill his ambition for an estate is a form of betrayal which leaves her in helpless loneliness: <br /><i>She learned the intricacy of loneliness: the horror of color, the roar of soundlessness, the menace of familiar objects lying still. …<br />Silence would fall like snow floating around her head and shoulders, spreading outward to wind-driven yet quiet leaves, dangling cowbells, the whack of Lina’s axe chopping firewood nearby. Her skin would flush, then chill. Sound would return eventually, but the loneliness might remain for days. Until, in the middle of it, he would ride up shouting.<br />“Where’s my star?”<br />“Here in the north,” she’d reply and he would toss a bolt of calico at her feet or hand her a packet of needles.</i><br /><br />I was most interested in Lina, who is the most self-reliant of the women. Despite her losses, she has the strongest foundation owing to having had a loving mother and identity forged through her connections of her tribe. The betrayal she feels lies in the blind destructiveness of the immigrant invaders:<br /><i>Her people had built sheltering cities for a thousand years and, except for the deathfeet of the Europes, might have built them for a thousand more. As it turned out the sachem had been dead wrong. The Europes neither fled nor died out. … They would come with languages that sounded like dog bark; with a childish hunger for animal fur. They would forever fence land, ship whole trees to faraway countries, take any women for quick pleasure, ruin soil, befoul sacred places and worship a dull, unimaginative god. …<br />Solitude would have crushed her had she not fallen into hermit skills and become one more thing that moved in the natural world. She cawed with birds, chatted with plants, spoke to squirrels, sang to the cow and opened her mouth to rain. The shame of having survived the destruction of her families shrank with her vow never to betray or abandon anyone she cherished.</i><br /><br />Sorrow is a strange bird with few skills to survive and an imaginary twin to ease her solitude. Having never known love, she is totally bowled over and obsessed with it when it happens to her, first with a free black man who periodically does blacksmith work for the farm and later with her child. The blacksmith betrays her by not committing to the relationship. The rapture of her love for him is portrayed so compellingly: <br /><i>The shine of water runs down your spine and I have shock at myself for wanting to lick there. I run away into the cowshed to stop this thing from happening inside me. Nothing stops it. There is only you. Nothing outside of you. My eyes not my stomach are the hungry pats of me. There will never be enough time to look how you move. Your arm goes up to strike iron. You drop to one knee. You bend. You stop to pour water first on the iron then down your throat. Before you know I am the world I am already kill by you. My mouth is open, my legs go softly and the heart is stretching to break.</i><br /><br />Florens comes off as the smartest of the women. She is the only one who can read, and she shows great courage and problem solving skills when tasked to make a long journey alone to get help from a herbalist when Jacob falls ill. Her mother made a sacrifice to give her up to Jacob instead of herself as a means of saving her from sexual assault by the master who owes Jacob money. Yet Florens is blind to that mercy and forever feels thrown away. <br /> <br />The audiobook came with a helpful interview with Morrison added at the end. She eloquently explains some of her intentions in portraying the uncertain future for people living in the new world, the inventive ad-hoc nature of its social forms, and the prevalence of slavery independent of racism. I see this is the seventh book by Morrison I have had the pleasure of experiencing. I see also that I gave each one 4 stars. Enough of such niggling. Who am I to deny this one five stars when it resonates so long after reading it. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3485255310032916196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maxwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maxwell" href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell">Maxwell</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/8132297-a-mercy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=book-club-pick">book-club-pick</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1381728-maxwell?shelf=black-authors">black-authors</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3073868636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17941720225914156123">[<b>8/10</b>]<br/><br/>What a beautifully heart-breaking book. It's a bit disorienting, jumping around from different characters' perspectives, and told in different writing styles. But I think that lends itself to the sort of medley of pain and struggle and sorrow these characters' face. Each has their own story to tell about loss, about displacement and about learning to live through it as best they can. And Morrison excellently captures those feelings without every feeling didactic. They are richly crafted an</span>
  <span id="freeText17941720225914156123" style="display:none">[<b>8/10</b>]<br /><br />What a beautifully heart-breaking book. It's a bit disorienting, jumping around from different characters' perspectives, and told in different writing styles. But I think that lends itself to the sort of medley of pain and struggle and sorrow these characters' face. Each has their own story to tell about loss, about displacement and about learning to live through it as best they can. And Morrison excellently captures those feelings without every feeling didactic. They are richly crafted and flawed characters who you can't help but root for. I think this one would reward a second reading after you've got the initial understanding of exactly <i>what</i> is happening, and upon re-read you can appreciate <i>how</i> she tells it so masterfully.</span>

<div id="review_68734784" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/68734784" />
    <a title="D. Pow" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow"><img alt="D. Pow" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339098707p2/2182799.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/68734784?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="D. Pow" class="user" itemprop="url" name="D. Pow" href="/user/show/2182799-d-pow">D. Pow</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer68734784&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer68734784" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer901720040188348603">Toni Morrison’s <i>A Mercy</i> is one of the most infuriating, lovely, haunted and haunting works I’ve read in many a moon. It is one of the few books I can remember that sent me back to read key passages and even whole chapters after I finished it to get clues to its maddeningly vague denouement and sample the blood-soaked, well-seeded soil of its prose one more time. I didn’t reread anything in frustration, but in gratitude and admiration. <br/><br/><i>A Mercy</i> is set ostensibly in the late 17th century, a fact I </span>
  <span id="freeText901720040188348603" style="display:none">Toni Morrison’s <i>A Mercy</i> is one of the most infuriating, lovely, haunted and haunting works I’ve read in many a moon. It is one of the few books I can remember that sent me back to read key passages and even whole chapters after I finished it to get clues to its maddeningly vague denouement and sample the blood-soaked, well-seeded soil of its prose one more time. I didn’t reread anything in frustration, but in gratitude and admiration. <br /><br /><i>A Mercy</i> is set ostensibly in the late 17th century, a fact I didn’t believe for a minute and yet strangely didn’t bother me in the least. While the book lacks the heavy weight of multiple and minute details that grant the usual work of historical fiction verisimilitude, it also doesn’t contain any howling anachronisms that hurl the reader out of the spell being cast. I feel more than a hard scrabble colonial era farm in an America barely shed of its birth caul, <i>A Mercy</i> is instead set in a place called ‘Morrisonland’, a gothic, sinister place suffused with lust, longing, confusions of identity and thwarted passions and improbable fates. It is a place that exists simultaneously only in the mind of its author and is a perfect representation of one aspect of America’s fucked up soul. It is a pagan place, not a Christian one, full of dark ritual, demons and a sprit-infused geography that telegraphs well impending tragedy for those with eyes to see. <br /><br />As in all Morrison books, race is central, skin colors of all grades on display throughout in a mad dance of slavery, ownership, parent and discarded child, victim and potential killer. But Morrison is too canny and wise to write a book that is a straight up indictment of the huge festering wound that is the American subjugation of African and Native American people. Everyone wrestles with their own slavery in this book, whether it is to ideas of mercantile ascension and social respect, loveless marriage, or thralldom to a land that will break bones before it yields anything that offers anything remotely resembling sustenance. <br />Morrison plants a few seeds of traditional romance within the narrative that point to the possibility of one character, the slave girl, Florens, escaping to a better fate via a freed African blacksmith and their intense physical relationship that develops. But once again Morrison is too true to her own darker gifts for any fairy tale ending where the princess is rescued and taken to some magic kingdom free of bitterness and strife. Instead, as in real life, a lesser fate and shadow identity is assumed where one that was hopefully headed for an upward trajectory is cast down utterly into blank confusion through her own befuddled mind and over-attachment to the lures of the body and a fantasy future that was always unlikely at best. <br /><br />It’s a tough ending, one if written by anyone other than Morrison might sparks of racism and sexism but the hard ending is foreshadowed with great effectiveness and anyone expecting all to end well in this stretch fly infested swampland wasn’t reading the text closely enough or else read too many Harlequins when they were younger. And while the ending is bitter and hard there are also grace notes of redemption, of life merely going on, of other wounded ones becoming ‘Complete’ that offer partial recompense for this harsh vision, making not wholly bleak, but tinged with small sweetness, like a flower bloomed in shadow. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="901720040188348603" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2957076926" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer515586677504206969">Short, tragic, beautifully written book set in the late 17th century in the American Colonies. It speaks of slavery, indentured servitude, patriarchy, exploitation, superstition, disease, and child mortality. Prominent themes include fear of abandonment, lack of agency, and unintended consequences. The author elucidates the seeds of issues that still have repercussions today. <br/><br/>Morrison focuses this book on an ensemble of characters. Jacob Vaark finds slavery abhorrent but, at the urging of her mo</span>
  <span id="freeText515586677504206969" style="display:none">Short, tragic, beautifully written book set in the late 17th century in the American Colonies. It speaks of slavery, indentured servitude, patriarchy, exploitation, superstition, disease, and child mortality. Prominent themes include fear of abandonment, lack of agency, and unintended consequences. The author elucidates the seeds of issues that still have repercussions today. <br /><br />Morrison focuses this book on an ensemble of characters. Jacob Vaark finds slavery abhorrent but, at the urging of her mother, accepts Florens as partial payment of a debt. She joins Lina, an indentured servant from a native tribe, and Sorrow, a mixed-race orphan that survives a shipwreck, in laboring at the Vaark’s farm. Jacob’s wife, Rebekka, arrives from London as what we would call a mail order bride. Scully and Willard are two male indentured servants whose servitude keeps getting extended by dubious means. A free African blacksmith plays a key role. Through this dream-like narrative, the reader learns the backstories of these characters. <br /><br />Morrison explores oppression based on gender, race, and class. She shows the heartbreak of mothers unable to protect their children. She writes expressively and packs a great deal into a slim novel. The sense of time and place is vivid. This is my first experience in reading Morrison’s work, and I look forward to reading more from her catalogue. <i>A Mercy</i> is an impressive book that conveys a powerful message in an artistic way. <br /></span>

<div id="review_3435486603" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3435486603?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trudie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trudie" href="/user/show/4617856-trudie">Trudie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10967146741893949684">This is only my second Toni Morrison after reading <i> Song of Solomon </i> last year, I guess I am tiptoeing cautiously towards her major works. <br/>It is extraordinarily impressive what Morrison manages to do here in this slim novel (167 pgs). The text rapidly immerses you in late 17th century America and almost all the characters are either orphans or foundlings. Essentially, one might classify this as a character study, each chapter tells one person's story of how they came to be alone in the world, an</span>
  <span id="freeText10967146741893949684" style="display:none">This is only my second Toni Morrison after reading <i> Song of Solomon </i> last year, I guess I am tiptoeing cautiously towards her major works. <br />It is extraordinarily impressive what Morrison manages to do here in this slim novel (167 pgs). The text rapidly immerses you in late 17th century America and almost all the characters are either orphans or foundlings. Essentially, one might classify this as a character study, each chapter tells one person's story of how they came to be alone in the world, and as the novel progresses, you begin to see what traits might allow them to survive. <br />It is so cleverly done. So much packed into a small space and, as I found with <i> Song of Solomon</i>, there is something quotable on almost every page. <br /><br /><i> Suddenly Jacob felt his stomach seize. The tobacco odor, so welcoming when he arrived, now nauseated him. Or was it the sugared rice, the hog cuts fried and dripping with molasses, the cocoa Lady D'Ortega was giddy about? Whatever it was, he couldn't stay there surrounded by a passel of slaves whose silence made him imagine an avalanche seen from a great distance. No sound, just the knowledge of a roar he could not hear. </i><br /><br />An impressive novel from one of America's greatest authors.</span>

<div id="review_27848145" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27848145" />
    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1006220-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310098934p2/1006220.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27848145?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/1006220-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer27848145&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27848145" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17640715239400277675">By the end of this novel I felt as though I had finished reading a collection of character sketches that could be used to form a much larger and perhaps more coherent text. Each chapter skips around from one character to another, and from first to third person narration, which in itself is not a problem, and if done well can make an interesting and eclectic whole. In this case, the text simply became frustrating; a puzzle that is frankly not interesting enough to put together. <br/><br/>The characters in </span>
  <span id="freeText17640715239400277675" style="display:none">By the end of this novel I felt as though I had finished reading a collection of character sketches that could be used to form a much larger and perhaps more coherent text. Each chapter skips around from one character to another, and from first to third person narration, which in itself is not a problem, and if done well can make an interesting and eclectic whole. In this case, the text simply became frustrating; a puzzle that is frankly not interesting enough to put together. <br /><br />The characters in this novel are diverse and could be very interesting if they were given the pages count they need. The characters consist of a Native American slave woman, a black slave woman who has a reputation of "madness," a young black slave girl whose sale to another man was urged on by her own mother, a pair of white atheists (who seem nice at first, but are really like all other whites, bad), a freed young blacksmith, and two indentured men. We learn bits and pieces about each of these people, but never enough to truly care about them. The small histories we are given whet the appetite for more details that are never delivered. After reading <i>Beloved</i> one gets spoiled by the closeness that we can have with characters like Sethe, Denver, and Paul D, but in <i>A Mercy</i> it felt that the characters were being held away from the reader, forcing them to be impersonal. Even in <i> Paradise</i> a novel filled with dozens of characters, the individuals at least seemed more alive than those in Morrison's new text. <br /><br />Now for the Narrative. I simply do not know what to say as there is so little there. What is the point of the novel? Where do the characters end up? There is little development, and the heart of the story spans perhaps a few days with backlogs giving us a little information about how these people ended up where they are, but the sparse journey and the little history given do not add up to an interesting whole. <br />Sorrow gives birth to a baby-girl and finds wholeness within that event, but we don't know her. The white woman Rebekka turns violent and cruel, but her betrayal means little because we hardly get to know the good in her which her cruelty is supposed to contrast. And yes, the young woman who was urged to be sold off by her mother is able to pick her sexual partner as her mother intended, but that freedom is so curtailed by the failure of her relationship and the reader's lack of personal connection with her that it barely matters. <br /><br />I wanted to love and adore this book as I do <i>Beloved,</i> but there is so little that the two books have in common. The utter intimacy that Morrison had to have developed with her characters in <i>Beloved</i> is not even attempted in this book. It is that distance that tears at my heart and makes me wonder if she will explore, and allow us readers to experience, that gut-wrenching sadness, anger, betrayal, again. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17640715239400277675" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_37538104" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37538104" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37538104?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37538104" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13850900978977664587">Yes, I am a <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved' title='Beloved by Toni Morrison' rel='nofollow noopener'>Toni Morrison</a> fan and believe she is incapable of writing a bad book, but that doesn't mean I wasn't ready to be critical of her new book if necessary. It's not necessary. The beginning may seem slow (that never bothers me) as we are thrust into a world that is faraway in time, but real. Historical details never bog down; they are worn lightly, as a reviewer put it.<br/><br/>Reviewers have compared one character here to Sethe from <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved' title='Beloved by Toni Morrison' rel='nofollow noopener'>Beloved</a>; and though I see the parallel, this is a very differen</span>
  <span id="freeText13850900978977664587" style="display:none">Yes, I am a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Toni Morrison</a> fan and believe she is incapable of writing a bad book, but that doesn't mean I wasn't ready to be critical of her new book if necessary. It's not necessary. The beginning may seem slow (that never bothers me) as we are thrust into a world that is faraway in time, but real. Historical details never bog down; they are worn lightly, as a reviewer put it.<br /><br />Reviewers have compared one character here to Sethe from <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a>; and though I see the parallel, this is a very different book. Morrison never repeats a book: each is different from the others. Here the setting is the U.S. before it was the U.S. and slavery is presented in all its forms: black, white, natives, indentured Europeans, 'rescued' orphans, those who are needy, etc. Life is difficult, dangerous and arduous for all, yet there are some who manage to make connections to others despite everything. A slim book that contains multitudes.<br /><br />The sign of a great book for me: I paged back to the beginning as I neared the end (as I approached the ending, I couldn't put it down) and when I was finished, I could've read it all over again. I will one day.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9453965519077934181" style="display:none">Maybe it's the bitter taste Beloved left me with; Maybe it's that she comes off as the poor woman's Maya Angelou; Maybe it's just that no matter how much I want to like her writing, I just can't. <br /><br />The first four chapters were confusing as hell and the remaining ones were disorienting. The POV's from chapter to chapter were so intertwined, I could barely remember who was talking and found myself constantly going back to the beginning of that particular chapter to double check. Not only that, but the timeline from chapter to chapter was muddled. Add to that nausea the style of writing. While the narrative was meant to be abrupt and disjointed, it actually came across as a sophomoric attempt to show a world that the writer herself stereotyped. <br /><br />With all that said, I did enjoy the ending of the book; the bird's eye view into the lives of the cast as it were to unfold and the purity that it conveyed. Though it felt like it took blood, sweat, and tears just to get there.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12531564871028090909">This was definitely not one of my favorites. I am usually a die-hard Morrison fan, but this one just wasn't up to par with her earlier works. Many people have compared this to Beloved, but I find that comparison unjust. This book, while it had its moments of brilliance, was inundated with dense, incomprehensible prose. At times, I was unable to decipher who was speaking and when. It just wasn't a good read for me.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16475912837209907496"> From my youngest sister, who reads often and prefers "Austenish" lit: "It was confusing and hard to get into and I didn't like the ending, but I did like that we heard every person's side of events. I still like my picks "A Tree Grows in Brooklyn" and "The Guernsey Potato Peel Pie Society" best."<br/><br/>From my middle sister, who is not a big reader and likes "family smut" (aka divorcee single mother who has had it hard and then finds love in the shape of a Tarzan woodsman living alone and horny in the</span>
  <span id="freeText16475912837209907496" style="display:none"> From my youngest sister, who reads often and prefers "Austenish" lit: "It was confusing and hard to get into and I didn't like the ending, but I did like that we heard every person's side of events. I still like my picks "A Tree Grows in Brooklyn" and "The Guernsey Potato Peel Pie Society" best."<br /><br />From my middle sister, who is not a big reader and likes "family smut" (aka divorcee single mother who has had it hard and then finds love in the shape of a Tarzan woodsman living alone and horny in the Rockies) and books like "the Shack" and "the Notebook": "This was the worst pick I have chosen so far. It took forever to know who was speaking and in the end nothing happened. This wasn't what I thought it would be based on the synopsis on Amazon...I thought it would be about a slave girl and her life. I liked my picks "<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1812457.The_Shack" title="The Shack by William P. Young" rel="nofollow noopener">The Shack</a>" and "The Memory Keepers Daughter more than this."<br /><br />From me, the literary hungry hippo who has read only "The Bluest Eye" from Toni in addition to "A Mercy": "Gave it almost 80 pages to really get going...chock full of things to analyze and the descriptions and format of the different voices provided early confusion but left a very layered tableau. I loved the ending- the last third of the book was great and really pulled together like strings twisting around and then forming a tight braid. Many similarities to "Bluest Eye"."<br /><br />I would love to discuss this book with you if you choose to read it...I have a theory snowballing around in my head about what the turning point of the story concerning the slave girl Florens.If you still have the book, look at the part where Florens escapes the cabin with the white girl's help- I think there is a line or two where Florens asks her if she is taken with the devil and the girl smiles and says yes and then Florens feels a spirit or wind or something follow or watch her through the fence. That part is where I thought she fully embraced her darker self, and then from there on she was a primal being, intent on embracing a heady darkness she couldn't control. The indulgence of herself in herself- the selfishness of it all (the blacksmith addresses this) was breathtaking and sudden.<br /><br />My younger sister thought the boy was just hurt. I thought she might've killed him, and my middle sister wasn't sure- she thought maybe both boy and man were killed or none were killed. What do you think?</span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13136606712489055983">I needed this. Morrison is always fascinating for me to read; I'm paying attention to the structure, the themes, the tone, and every nuance she wrings out of her perfect sentences.<br/><br/>This is one of her easier novels, one featuring a swirling ensemble of voices, all gazing at the harsh reality of a group of women living in 17th century America. <br/><br/>The whole novel is one fluid tapestry, quite intimate, with a subtlety that is rare for Morrison but which works so well here. She questions how, in a world </span>
  <span id="freeText13136606712489055983" style="display:none">I needed this. Morrison is always fascinating for me to read; I'm paying attention to the structure, the themes, the tone, and every nuance she wrings out of her perfect sentences.<br /><br />This is one of her easier novels, one featuring a swirling ensemble of voices, all gazing at the harsh reality of a group of women living in 17th century America. <br /><br />The whole novel is one fluid tapestry, quite intimate, with a subtlety that is rare for Morrison but which works so well here. She questions how, in a world where one is inevitably owned in some way, do you form a self that is free of dominion? <br /><br />Through the novel's incredibly strong female characters, she answers this question in several intense and surprising ways. I was especially moved by the final chapter, which I think really explains the entire point of the novel. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13136606712489055983" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_38864234" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38864234" />
    <a title="Sandi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811687-sandi"><img alt="Sandi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404709147p2/811687.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/38864234?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sandi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sandi" href="/user/show/811687-sandi">Sandi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811687-sandi?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer38864234" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8972266128087623401">Dear Ms. Morrison:<br/><br/>I just want you to know that I think you are a wonderful writer. I remember picking up a copy of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11337.The_Bluest_Eye' title='The Bluest Eye by Toni Morrison' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Bluest Eye</a> back in 1990 because I was taking a stupid college course and we were required to read a book by a female author written after WWII. I chose your book because it was really short and I didn't want to put a lot of time into that assignment. I remember crying while reading it and wanting to take that little girl out of her miserable life and make her feel better about h</span>
  <span id="freeText8972266128087623401" style="display:none">Dear Ms. Morrison:<br /><br />I just want you to know that I think you are a wonderful writer. I remember picking up a copy of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11337.The_Bluest_Eye" title="The Bluest Eye by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bluest Eye</a> back in 1990 because I was taking a stupid college course and we were required to read a book by a female author written after WWII. I chose your book because it was really short and I didn't want to put a lot of time into that assignment. I remember crying while reading it and wanting to take that little girl out of her miserable life and make her feel better about herself. When my son was a baby, I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Beloved</a> and was equally horrified and sympathetic to Sethe's predicament. I loved the depth of emotion that book brought out in me. Later, I read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/37398.Jazz__Contemporary_Fiction__Plume_" title="Jazz (Contemporary Fiction, Plume) by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Jazz</a> and was so impressed by the way the language echoed the theme. The rhythm of your words was so, well, jazzy.<br /><br />I really, really wanted to give <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3009435.A_Mercy" title="A Mercy by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">A Mercy</a> more than three stars. I wanted to be blown away. I was looking forward to really feeling something, even if it didn't feel good. Unfortunately, the only things I felt were confusion and disappointment. It took me half of each chapter to figure out which character the chapter was about and what time frame the chapter was set in. It felt flat and pointless. I never got to really know any of the characters. Honestly, 167 pages is not enough to cover the number of characters and ideas this story is trying to convey. It's more like a draft than a finished novel. It's an epic story that's been condensed and abridged. I wanted to know more about these characters and the 17th century American colonies in which they lived. I liked that nobody was either a hero or a villain, but human. I just would have liked to know these humans better. <br /><br />Frankly, the reason I do give your book as many as three stars is that the prose is absolutely beautiful, as usual for you. The imagery and the rhythm is both poetic and musical. I just wish there had been more of it.<br /><br />Sincerely,<br />Sandi K. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8972266128087623401" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8646517661145578749">"You say I am wilderness. I am. Is that a tremble on your mouth, in your eye? Are you afraid? You should be." <br/><br/>Written in different POV's Morrison's writing is intricate, detailed, and requires close reading to be able to understand. A neo-slave narrative, that's less about slavery itself and more about the real brutality during this time: that to lose ones origins, kinship, and family is to lose ones sense of self. </span>

<div id="review_2398828743" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2398828743" />
    <a title="La Tonya  Jordan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32053623-la-tonya-jordan"><img alt="La Tonya  Jordan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1501465115p2/32053623.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2398828743?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="La Tonya  Jordan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="La Tonya  Jordan" href="/user/show/32053623-la-tonya-jordan">La Tonya  Jordan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Everyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to La Tonya  by: </span>
      Myself 
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32053623-la-tonya-jordan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2398828743" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3805672020805398368">What can I say about this marvelous read, the poetry of the language is music to my ears. I can feel the anguish of those owned and indentured to others. The desire for love and family no matter how it comes to you. The agony of wanting freedom others have or what others appear to have in their possession. Desperately wanting to hold onto memories of Africa, villages, land, and customs ripped from you. Distressed over circumstances you cannot control. <br/><br/>This is a story of a young girl named Floren</span>
  <span id="freeText3805672020805398368" style="display:none">What can I say about this marvelous read, the poetry of the language is music to my ears. I can feel the anguish of those owned and indentured to others. The desire for love and family no matter how it comes to you. The agony of wanting freedom others have or what others appear to have in their possession. Desperately wanting to hold onto memories of Africa, villages, land, and customs ripped from you. Distressed over circumstances you cannot control. <br /><br />This is a story of a young girl named Florens who was traded to Jacob Vaark, an Anglo-Dutch trader, to settle a debt from a plantation owner. Florens mother, a prize slave, offered her to Vaark. He takes her far away north from her home where she meets Lina, Sorrow, and Mistress, Rebekka, Vaark's wife. She does find warmth and friendship among this household. She believes she has found a home until Vaark dies and circumstances changes like the weather and the wind. Read to find out the secrets not shared. The resentments held deep within the soul. Hear the cries for help no one sees......... A beautiful read. <br /><br />Quote: <br /><br />Fined five thousand pounds of tobacco by the Lord Proprietarys' magistrate for throwing their bodies too close to the bay; forced to scoop up the corpes-those they could find (they used pikes and nets, D'Ortega said, a purchase which itself cost two pounds, six)---and ordered to burn or bury them. <br /><br />That God hated idleness most of all, so staring off into space to weep for a mother or a playmate was to court damnation. <br /><br />When Lina tried to enlighten her, saying, "You are one leaf on his tree," Florens shook her head, closed her eyes and replied, "No. I am his tree." <br /><br />It is one matter to live in your own waste; it is another to live in another's.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3805672020805398368" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
